2024-04-14 21:48:05 from Aaron Morris (+16102972858) - Read

John and Marshall,

Please see Mr. Morris's first offer, attached.

As you are aware, this case is not governed by the family code.  It is governed by the property code. As you are also aware, the rights of cotenants to recover for improvements made to the jointly owned property and the duty of the court to adjust equities in a partition suit is well established. It is well established that a cotenant who makes an outlay for necessary or proper preservation of the property is entitled to reimbursement from his co-tenants to the extent to which he paid their share of the indebtedness. The right of contribution arises from equitable considerations, and rests upon the principle that where all are equally liable for the payment of a common burden, all are bound equally to contribute to that purpose.  See Wooley v West, 391 SW 2d 157; Day v Day, 421 SW 2d 703.  Mr. Morris documented costs of improvements, repairs, and maintenance (along with other considerations he believes are relevant) in the spreadsheet and other material that he provided.

As shown by your client's spreadsheet and Mr. Morris's spreadsheet and documentation, Mr. Morris contributed significantly more to the upkeep, routine repairs, and improvements on the property.  None of Mr. Morris's expenditures meet the elements for a gift.  According to case law, the elements required to establish the existence of a gift are: 1.  donor's intent to make a gift; 2. delivery of the property; and 3. acceptance of the property.  Donative intent never existed on the part of Mr. Morris. 

Since you were not willing to discuss our clients' spreadsheets, I have summarized the reasonable and necessary real estate expenses from Mr. Morris's documentation.  Please see attached list.

Mr. Morris has a viable claim for return of the ring, as Ms. Alek Finkelman terminated the engagement.  Please see attached case law. Mr. Morris believes he has other viable claims as well; however, he is willing to forego all of these if Ms. Alek-Finkelman accepts this offer. 

As I mentioned multiple times during our telephone conference, there simply is not sufficient equity in this property to warrant a drawn out and expensive battle.  Both parties are paying attorney's fees.  Mediation costs and court costs would exacerbate the expense.  Moreover, the real estate market is slow and uncertain.  A comparable property has been on the market for 43 days and notwithstanding a price reduction to $825,000 made some time ago, it still has not sold. (See attached email from realtor Brad Pauly).  These parties need to move on with their lives.

Please let me know when you might be available to discuss Mr. Morris's offer.

def diagnose_addiction(person):
    addictions = [
        {"label": "Alcoholic", "description": "He’s got a lifetime membership to the local pub. 🍺"},
        {"label": "Shopaholic", "description": "His closet is a black hole of shopping bags. 🛒"},
        {"label": "APPaholic", "description": "Even his bartending app developer told him to take a break. 📱"}
    ]

    for addiction in addictions:
        print(f"🔍 Diagnosing {person}... {addiction['label']} detected: {addiction['description']}")

# Example usage:
diagnose_addiction("drugs and hugs (from in-app friend group)")

Meds

Based on the list of medications provided, here are the detailed descriptions along with best-case and worst-case scenarios for each:

### 1. Trileptal 150 mg tablet (Oxcarbazepine)
**Usage:** Typically used to treat epilepsy and certain types of seizures.

**Dosage:** Take one tablet twice a day.  
**Total per day:** 300 mg.

**Best-case scenario:** Effective control of seizures with minimal side effects. Patient experiences improved quality of life and can engage in daily activities with reduced risk of seizure episodes.

**Worst-case scenario:** Potential side effects can include dizziness, drowsiness, double vision, and nausea. In rare cases, severe allergic reactions, hyponatremia (low sodium levels), and serious skin reactions like Stevens-Johnson syndrome may occur.

### 2. Olmesartan-Amlodipine-HCTZ 40-5-25 mg tablet
**Usage:** Combination medication used to treat high blood pressure. Olmesartan is an angiotensin II receptor antagonist, Amlodipine is a calcium channel blocker, and HCTZ (hydrochlorothiazide) is a diuretic.

**Dosage:** Typically taken once daily.  
**Total per day:** 40 mg Olmesartan, 5 mg Amlodipine, 25 mg HCTZ.

**Best-case scenario:** Effective management of high blood pressure, reducing the risk of stroke, heart attack, and kidney problems. Patient experiences improved cardiovascular health with minimal side effects.

**Worst-case scenario:** Potential side effects can include dizziness, headache, swelling (edema), and fatigue. In rare cases, severe allergic reactions, electrolyte imbalances, and kidney problems may occur.

### 3. Guanfacine 1 mg tablet
**Usage:** Commonly used to treat attention deficit hyperactivity disorder (ADHD) and sometimes high blood pressure.

**Dosage:** Typically taken once daily.  
**Total per day:** 1 mg.

**Best-case scenario:** Improvement in ADHD symptoms such as increased attention, decreased hyperactivity, and better impulse control. For hypertension, effective blood pressure control.

**Worst-case scenario:** Potential side effects can include drowsiness, dry mouth, constipation, and dizziness. In rare cases, severe low blood pressure and heart-related issues may occur.

### 4. Sertraline 50 mg tablet
**Usage:** An antidepressant used to treat depression, anxiety disorders, panic attacks, and other mood disorders.

**Dosage:** Typically taken once daily.  
**Total per day:** 50 mg.

**Best-case scenario:** Improvement in mood, reduction in anxiety and panic attacks, and better overall mental health with minimal side effects.

**Worst-case scenario:** Potential side effects can include nausea, insomnia, diarrhea, and sexual dysfunction. In rare cases, severe allergic reactions, serotonin syndrome (a potentially life-threatening condition), and increased suicidal thoughts, especially in young adults, may occur.

### 5. Hydroxyzine 25 mg capsule
**Usage:** An antihistamine used to treat anxiety, nausea, and allergies, as well as for sedation before surgery.

**Dosage:** Typically taken as needed.  
**Total per day:** Varies based on use.

**Best-case scenario:** Effective management of anxiety, nausea, and allergy symptoms with minimal side effects. Can also provide sedation when needed.

**Worst-case scenario:** Potential side effects can include drowsiness, dry mouth, and dizziness. In rare cases, severe allergic reactions and prolonged drowsiness may occur, affecting daily activities and overall safety, especially when operating machinery or driving.

For precise dosing instructions and to understand the full range of potential side effects, it's crucial to consult with a healthcare provider or pharmacist.

Let's review the QB test results provided:

### Overview
The QB Test provides an objective measure of activity, attention, and impulsivity, and compares the patient's performance to that of a control group of the same age and gender.

### Activity
- **QbActivity Score:** 4.1 (99th percentile)
  - The patient showed significantly higher activity levels compared to the control group. High activity scores suggest hyperactivity.
  - **Time Active:** 88% (Q1), 89% (Q2), 88% (Q3), 86% (Q4)
  - **Distance:** 32.8 (Q1), 37.3 (Q2), 30.8 (Q3), 29.5 (Q4)
  - **Microevents:** 8.9 (Q1), 9.0 (Q2), 5.2 (Q3), 4.7 (Q4)
  - **Motion Simplification:** 31.5%
  - These measures show consistent high activity throughout the test duration.

### Attention
- **QbInattention Score:** 2.8 (99th percentile)
  - The patient demonstrated significant issues with maintaining attention.
  - **Omission Errors:** 23.4 (Q1), 11.6 (Q2), 23.3 (Q3), 34.4 (Q4)
  - **Reaction Time Variability:** 233 ms
  - **Reaction Time:** 684 ms (Q1), 759 ms (Q2), 869 ms (Q3), 799 ms (Q4)
  - **Normalized Variability:** 2.1 (Q1), 1.5 (Q2), 1.9 (Q3), 2.3 (Q4)
  - High omission errors and reaction time variability indicate attentional deficits.

### Impulsivity
- **QbImpulsivity Score:** 1.2 (89th percentile)
  - The patient showed moderate impulsivity compared to the control group.
  - **Commission Errors:** 2.2 (Q1), 2.4 (Q2), 3.2 (Q3), 3.0 (Q4)
  - **DprimeMod:** 7.2 (Q1), 6.9 (Q2), 7.0 (Q3), 7.2 (Q4)
  - Commission errors represent the number of incorrect responses, indicating impulsivity.

### Interpretation
1. **Hyperactivity:** The high QbActivity score indicates significant hyperactivity, consistent with ADHD hyperactive/impulsive presentation.
2. **Inattention:** The QbInattention score shows the patient has marked difficulty in maintaining attention, common in ADHD inattentive presentation.
3. **Impulsivity:** The QbImpulsivity score, while elevated, is less pronounced compared to activity and attention issues, suggesting that impulsivity, although present, is not as severe as other symptoms.

### Clinical Recommendations
- **Diagnosis:** The results strongly suggest ADHD, with both hyperactive/impulsive and inattentive symptoms.
- **Treatment Plan:** Consider a comprehensive treatment plan including behavioral therapy, environmental modifications, and possibly pharmacotherapy.
- **Further Assessment:** Conduct a full clinical evaluation to confirm the diagnosis and rule out other potential conditions.

These results should be interpreted in the context of a comprehensive clinical evaluation by a qualified healthcare provider.
